Hormuz Blockade, Meta's Muse & AI in Healthcare

Special Competitive Studies Project(92.3K)·84:46·233 views·2026-05-02
Jensen HuangCEO, NVIDIA
TL;DRAs the Strait of Hormuz conflict enters Day 63 amidst severe regional diplomatic shifts and staggering economic damage to Iran, the global tech race intensifies with Beijing blocking Meta's $2 billion acquisition of AI startup Manus, the US Departmen...
TL;DR As the Strait of Hormuz conflict enters Day 63 amidst severe regional diplomatic shifts and staggering economic damage to Iran, the global tech race intensifies with Beijing blocking Meta's $2 billion acquisition of AI startup Manus, the US Department of Defense signing...

TL;DR

As the Strait of Hormuz conflict enters Day 63 amidst severe regional diplomatic shifts and staggering economic damage to Iran, the global tech race intensifies with Beijing blocking Meta's $2 billion acquisition of AI startup Manus, the US Department of Defense signing major cloud/AI contracts for classified systems, and the release of Meta’s highly personalized "Muse Spark" model.

Geopolitical Stalemate and Economic Shock in the Strait of Hormuz

  • The Diplomatic Stalemate in Islamabad: Negotiations to reopen the Strait of Hormuz have stalled as Washington and Tehran clash over deal packaging.
      • Bundled vs. Clean Deals: Iran proposed a "clean deal" to lift the US blockade in exchange for opening the strait, parking nuclear negotiations for later. Conversely, President Trump insists on bundling the nuclear file and strait access into a single package.
      • Failed Talks: Expectation of a round-two meeting in Islamabad fell through when Iran refused to negotiate while the blockade remains active, forcing US negotiators Steve Witkoff and Jared Kushner to transition to phone-only diplomacy.
  • Tectonic Regional Shifts and Financial Retaliation: The mediation process has exposed deep fractures between Gulf nations, specifically Saudi Arabia and the UAE.
      • Emirati Disapproval: Unhappy with Pakistan's Saudi-endorsed mediation efforts, the UAE withdrew a $3.5 billion central bank deposit rollover from Pakistan (equal to one-fifth of Pakistan's reserves).
      • Saudi Intervention: Saudi Arabia quickly neutralized Pakistan's immediate financial crisis with a $3 billion emergency package.
      • Alternative Security Alignments: The UAE is shifting toward Israeli-US partnerships, housing Israeli troops and operating IDF Iron Beam systems, while Saudi Arabia hedges with Qatar, Turkey, and Pakistan.
  • Iran's Catastrophic Economic Decline: While the Iranian regime portrays resilience, internal data reveals the devastating toll of the US blockade instituted on April 13.
      • Labor and Inflation: Iran has suffered 1 million immediate job losses, with 12 million more at risk (half of the national labor force), alongside annual inflation spiking to 70%.
      • Business Destruction: Approximately 23,000 businesses and manufacturing facilities have been destroyed, leaving Iran with an estimated $300 billion reconstruction bill.
  • Adversary Lessons and US Military Readiness: Competitors like China and Russia are closely monitoring the conflict for strategic insights.
      • AI in Combat: Observers are noting the US military's highly successful multi-domain campaign, which integrated AI for the first time in combat to prosecute over 10,000 targets within the first 24 hours.
      • US Budgetary Signals: The US has proposed an unprecedented $1.5 trillion defense budget (5% of GDP) for the upcoming fiscal year.
      • Supply Drawdowns: Analysts note that Beijing is closely monitoring the drawdown of US stockpiles of long-range precision-strike missiles, factoring replenishment times into their own regional calculations.

China's Aggressive Tech Protectionism and the AI Frontier

  • Beijing Blocks Meta's $2 Billion Manus Acquisition: Underlining its stance that AI talent is a national resource, Beijing has officially blocked Meta’s acquisition of AI startup Manus.
      • "Singapore-Washing" Defeated: Manus had rebranded itself as a Singaporean company to access Western markets and capital, but Chinese authorities claimed regulatory jurisdiction and placed an exit ban on the founders.
      • A Stark Message: The block signals that Chinese developers—regardless of international relocation—remain under Beijing's strict control, ending a viable "exit strategy" for Chinese founders aiming for Western acquisitions.
  • DeepSeek V4 Release and the Open-Weight Race: One year after the market-disrupting R1 model, Chinese developer DeepSeek launched DeepSeek V4, though to a more muted global reception.
      • Cost-Capability Gap: V4 is a highly accessible open-weight model, but its capabilities fall short of top-tier US frontier models like GPT-5.5 or Claude Opus 4.5, while being priced higher than other domestic Chinese competitors.
      • Hardware Smuggling and Optimization: Although the model's technical paper claims optimization for Huawei Ascend chips, US intelligence suggests the model was trained using smuggled, top-of-the-line Nvidia Blackwell chips.

US AI Infrastructure Realities and Federal Adoption

  • DoD Solidifies Major Commercial AI Partnerships: The US Department of Defense has signed major contracts with Nvidia, Microsoft, Amazon Web Services (AWS), and Google to deploy commercial AI capabilities into highly secure, classified systems.
      • Vulnerability Patching: The government is focusing heavily on integrating specialized models like Anthropic's "Mythos" to automate vulnerability identification and system hardening ahead of adversary cyber capabilities.
  • Maine Governor Vetoes Statewide Data Center Pause: Highlighting the fierce domestic debate over AI's massive resource demands, Maine's Governor Janet Mills vetoed a bipartisan bill that would have instituted a statewide moratorium on large-scale data centers.
      • Economic vs. Resource Tradeoffs: While agreeing with the bill's environmental and grid-protection principles, the Governor vetoed it because it did not exempt a planned $550 million brownfield data center project in Jay, Maine, which promised major economic revitalization for a struggling mill town.
      • Brownfield Models: The conflict highlighted a potential compromise path: repurposing vacant, historic industrial mills for digital infrastructure to win local community backing.

AI-Driven Breakthroughs in Consumer Tech and Life Sciences

  • Meta Launches Muse Spark for Personal Intelligence: Developed in a record nine months from lab formation to release, Meta’s new Muse Spark model shifts the AI focus from office productivity to everyday lifestyle management.
      • Personalized Agentism: Designed to operate as a personalized assistant directly within Meta's ecosystem (reaching 3.5 billion users), the model emphasizes multimodal context-awareness, natural language integration, and digital execution of lifestyle tasks (e.g., shopping, event planning).
      • Frontier Risk Mitigation: Alongside the launch, Meta published a comprehensive 200-page safety and preparedness report addressing chemical, biological, cyber, and loss-of-control risks.
  • Reigniting the Discovery Engine for Cures: AI is acting as a massive productivity booster for a life sciences sector currently plagued by soaring drug development costs.
      • Widespread Research Usage: Over 200,000 researchers worldwide now use ChatGPT weekly to fast-track literature reviews, drafting, and data synthesis, shifting weeks of manual searching into minutes.
      • The $500M Virtual Cell Initiative: The Chan Zuckerberg Biohub announced a massive $500 million investment to build predictive, AI-driven virtual models of human biology down to the individual cell level, aiming to replicate the success of protein-folding predictions at scale.

Notable Quotes

"It's a critical path for U.S. AI supremacy to have American AI adopted all over the world by consumers, and that's exactly what we're doing." — *Victoria, Meta Representative*
"We're in a renegotiation of almost everything in terms of the transatlantic relationship... But without a transatlantic basis, there's no really stable international order." — *John Bew, Former UK National Security Advisor*
"Writing is so hard. I hate writing. But, you know, with AI, it was tolerable." — *Jensen Huang, Nvidia CEO*
"The UAE sees its future very much firmly in the Israeli and American camp, whereas Saudi Arabia is hedging more with Qatar, Turkey, Pakistan." — *Humza Jilani, Financial Times Correspondent*

While President Trump wants to have nuclear and the strait bundled into the same package. And actually, this debate over whether nuclear and the strait can go together was one of the reasons why some of the expectations that there would be a round two of talks in Islamabad last weekend fell through. Iranian Foreign Minister Araghchi announced that he was coming to Islamabad for a bilateral trip and then shortly thereafter President Trump announced that he was sending Steve Witkoff and Jared Kushner for a round two of negotiations, but the Iranians made it clear that they will not sit down while the blockade is in place. And in the end, those talks did not happen. President Trump canceled the expected trip by his negotiators and said, "We're only going to talk by phone now, through Pakistan or through other channels." And what we're left with basically is a bit of a stalemate where Iran is saying that they, you know, they don't want to talk about nuclear right now. Whereas if Trump is left with a situation where the strait is reopened and the blockade is lifted without any progress on nuclear, then he's left with charges that he's finishing this war without the core job being done on the nuclear file. And you're there on the ground, so how is the atmosphere there? I mean, the atmosphere has definitely gyrated between a lot of excitement. I think they thought there was going to be a deal last weekend. Field Marshal Asim Munir, Foreign Minister Ishaq Dar, and Prime Minister Shehbaz Sharif, along with the ISI director Asim Malik are still constantly on the phone with their Iranian and American counterparts, trying to piece together whatever gaps are still left and try and push forward a deal. They still say that, you know, a deal is possible. We think that we're closing in on a deal, however, Pakistan has its motivations for wanting to be optimistic here because the other side of a world where there isn't a deal is one where Pakistan, one of the most economically vulnerable countries to the effects of the closure of the strait, faces major challenges. A country with a mutual defense pact with Saudi Arabia that in a renewed set of hostilities could commit it to entering the war, those are basically dire circumstances that they think are unthinkable. And so they obviously are trying to push as hard as they can to get a deal. We saw sort of signs of that existential desperation that they felt when it seemed like a deal might be falling through, but they've been able to salvage at least a new proposal to the Americans and keep diplomacy alive. People in the region actually fear this. As tourism has dried up, as their ability to export oil through the strait has also dried up—I mean they have an alternative pipeline, but they really want to see this as a way to end their disputes with Iran. On the internet clampdown, I also saw that all the online businesses are suffering inside Iran because of the internet clampdown. In particular, those businesses that relied on the internet have been impacted most severely, and they probably account for those 1 million job losses that took place in the immediate aftermath of the war. So this is a country that had been under pressure from sanctions and previous actions for many years, and this war I think has just introduced a significant shock to their economy. I'm happy to go through sort of the US economic data in contrast, which show the signs of a very robust economy and not one that's being impacted by the increase in the price of oil. So I think time is on the US side. Yeah, I mean, I think that's an accurate portrayal of Iran's economy. I think the IMF is now predicting what, a 6% decline at least this year for the economy, which is absolutely devastating. I mean, it's entirely possible that Iran is inching closer and closer and closer to strategic exhaustion. However, I've contended from the beginning that Iran's center of gravity is not their economy. And it's not public opinion. It's the cohesiveness of the IRGC and so far that shows no signs of cracking. The regime does not fear their public. They don't fear public protests. They fear losing the ability to suppress those protests, and so far that hasn't happened. So Ylber, Chip, our adversaries are watching this crisis unfold. What lessons do you think Pyongyang, Beijing, and Moscow are drawing about US leverage, but also some of the challenges that the United States is facing in this crisis? I think the first lesson that they're drawing is that the US political will is strong to undertake military action. So I think the actions against Iran have shaken that assumption. So that's one. Second, I think it's hard not to be impressed with the multi-domain campaign that the US leveraged there on Iran, including the use of AI for the first time in combat to prosecute over 10,000 targets just in the first 24 hours. So I think if you're the US adversaries, I think you would take a hard look at whether you would want to challenge the US interests anywhere around the world at this time. Mindful obviously that the Iran campaign came in the aftermath of the operation against Venezuela, the downfall of the Assad regime in Syria, as well as a number of other steps. He also mentioned the Russian assistance to Iran. And just a reminder, the US has put forward the proposal for a 1.5 trillion dollar budget, so that would be 5% of GDP next year for defense spending. Unprecedented levels that we haven't seen probably in decades. So that will introduce significant capabilities. It brings the US to the level that the US has insisted NATO allies meet. So I think that's another sort of indication I would look at in terms of where the US military strength and capabilities are headed. Victoria, this moves the model more from a chatbot to like a personalized assistant basically. How have the policymakers in D.C. reacted to this model in your engagements with folks here in D.C.? Yeah, very positively. So one of the things that has happened is that as the performance of our models has scaled, so has our approach to safety. And so we released, I think it clocks in at almost 200 pages, the safety and preparedness report that goes into quite a lot of detail around the testing and evaluations that we did related to frontier risk. So when it comes to chemical and biological, cyber, loss of control, those sorts of issues. But also things like addressing political bias and harmful content. We're really proud of the work that we've done there and we're leading the industry across a lot of vectors. Yeah. This was—I mean—the first model since you created the new lab under Alex, who is a friend of ours, leadership. What do you predict are the next steps to build on top of this model? Yeah, so we're just getting started, which I'm really excited about. As the King stood before Congress this week, did you see that visit as a return to the old rules-based order or was it a strategic attempt to stabilize an alliance that is effectively, you know, moving away from each other? I think there are challenges in the transatlantic alliance. There was a bit of controversy with regard to the King's visit. I thought the King's speech itself articulated something about the transatlantic relationship that's not simply dependent on the post-1945 story but one that goes far deeper than that as well. In that regard, I thought it was a pretty deftly handled visit. I mean, I watch these things closely, I have some experience of them from my time in government, and I just think, you know, the words chosen both for the formal part of the occasion in Congress but also the more informal repartee and jokes in the subsequent part of the visit were also done in a pretty well and accomplished way. In your opinion, do you think the special relationship can survive as Washington moves towards more a mercantilist approach to the world order? And London is really debating whether or not they should increase defense spending, their role in the world, their role in sort of our bilateral relationship. Yeah, I do. I mean, the first thing to say is, and we admit this only in friendly company in the US, a lot of the British Prime Ministers I worked for or the political or diplomatic establishment, we're under no illusions as to what the special relationship is. I mean, it's a kind of concept crafted by Churchill in a wartime context, reiterated when he was out of office actually in his famous Fulton speech. It doesn't and never has been based on pure sentiment. It's been based on a certain community of values but also, you know, quite hard interests and a sense in which our interests are better served by working together. We in the UK are the junior partner in that relationship. The US is going through a shift in how it perceives the world, and the US is getting more engaged in different parts of the world like the Indo-Pacific. Someone said the other day, actually our ambassador in an off-guarded private setting, that the US has a special relationship with Israel, for example. So over here we don't need to be patted on the back with this sort of rhetoric about the special relationship, but substantively I think it really matters. Look, it really matters for the UK, it really matters for a series of major European countries—you take the Poles, you take the Germans in particular as two countries that really bind and invest into the security line. I think that transatlantic relationship has a long way to run. So I take this as a policymaker or a former advisor but also as a historian and I, actually if you look at the hard metrics, US-EU trade for example has never been higher. In my own view, without a transatlantic basis, there's no really stable international order as well. If you look at the US national security strategy or what's said, I mean there's plenty that people in Europe recoil at, but it has a deep psychological investment in Europe and says the future of Europe is really important to us. Now lots of people in Europe don't like that version of the future they're being presented, but so I think the transatlantic relationship is vital. That's not to say the Indo-Pacific, Asia-Pacific is not more important to the United States. But the way I characterize the current moment is, I don't do, as you know, Ylli, we've known each other for years, hyperbole and drama and crying into my spilled milk or whatever the analogy is and you have these successive end of the liberal international order pieces or speeches at Davos or the Financial Times. I characterize the current moment as follows: I think we're in a renegotiation of almost everything in terms of the terms of the transatlantic relationship. So that means the inputs we have, relative levels of defense spending for example, the geographic focus of that defense spending or force posture, the import controls, export controls, the tariffs, relative tariffs between us as well. It's quite a fraught and difficult renegotiation, but you would hope through the period of renegotiation you would actually, it's not beyond the wit of man to find an equilibrium on the other side of it, and I think that might be a 10-year process, who knows. But I think there's plenty in terms of the metrics of economic relationships, technological relationships, defense relationships that will carry us through this period of turbulence. We're not going to kid ourselves that we are the swing vote in your calculations, but I would suggest it's still a majority in Washington, D.C. who think, even if our allies aren't as powerful or as effective as we might like them, it's better to have allies and partners with us who are aligned on values, who are aligned on how we should deploy that technology, who are able to fight alongside us, frankly who have the political will to fight alongside us when those contingencies happen as well. So, you know, look I'm not in despair and you highlighted a list of things that are important. It's hard, it's difficult and it's costly, but that is a kind of direction of travel. The smart people you talk to in these different European national security straits are running both of those things simultaneously. Ken Griffin - Founder and CEO of Citadel | Investment Conference 2026

Norges Bank Investment Management(107.0K)·17:17·7.9K views·2026-05-02
Ken GriffinFounder and CEO, Citadel
TL;DRCitadel’s success is built on an unapologetic "winning" culture that prioritizes rapid, decentralized decision-making and a disciplined approach to technology where high-impact execution is favored over experimental breadth.
Citadel’s success is built on an unapologetic "winning" culture that prioritizes rapid, decentralized decision-making and a disciplined approach to technology where high-impact execution is favored over experimental breadth. The firm views human capital as its primary product,...

TL;DR

Citadel’s success is built on an unapologetic "winning" culture that prioritizes rapid, decentralized decision-making and a disciplined approach to technology where high-impact execution is favored over experimental breadth. The firm views human capital as its primary product, focusing on developing resilient leaders who aspire to long-term impact rather than short-term financial exits.

Organizational Agility and Decision-Making

  • Negative Consent Model: To prevent management from becoming a bottleneck, Griffin employs a "no news is good news" policy for decision-making.
      • If a subordinate seeks input and does not receive a response within a specified window (e.g., 48–96 hours), the decision is considered approved.
      • This grants junior employees agency and prevents the firm from losing its startup-like speed as it scales.
  • Empowering Junior Talent: Internal principles dictate that the person closest to the information—often the most junior—should be the one making the decision.
      • While this risks errors, it accelerates the learning curve and fosters a culture of accountability.
  • Managing the "Bureaucratic State": As firms grow in regulatory complexity, Griffin emphasizes the need to aggressively control the size of the bureaucracy to maintain execution speed.

Strategic Talent Development

  • CEO-Level Performance Audits: Griffin spends several weeks annually reviewing thousands of employee assessments personally.
      • Focus on "Failure to Flourish": He looks for patterns where new hires (from PhDs to lateral transfers) are not succeeding to identify firm-wide gaps in professional development or trust.
  • The "Winner's Mosaic" in Hiring: There is no single interview question; instead, Citadel looks for a "mosaic" of traits centered on aspiration and resilience.
      • Aspiration over Ambition: Griffin seeks candidates who want to build and lead businesses rather than those with a "magic number" (e.g., $10 million) at which they intend to quit.
      • Competitive Backgrounds: The firm values those who have competed in high-stakes arenas like sports, music, or debate because it proves they can handle the "agony of defeat" and return to work.
  • Resilience as a Core Competency: In quantitative investing, even top portfolio managers are only "right" about 53% of the time.
      • Success requires the mental fortitude to handle being "wrong and humbled" frequently without losing productivity.

Technology Adoption: Bleeding vs. Leading Edge

  • Disciplined GenAI Integration: Rather than allowing "random ad hoc projects," Griffin recently pruned 200 internal GenAI initiatives down to five high-priority projects.
      • This prevents distraction and ensures resources are concentrated on transformative use cases.
  • The "Bleeding Edge" vs. "Leading Edge" Framework: Management must decide with volition when to be a pioneer and when to be a "fast follower."
      • Bleeding Edge: Used for core breakthroughs like TensorFlow, where Citadel was in production within four weeks of the tool’s release.
      • Leading Edge: Used when it is more cost-effective to let others "pave the way" and then capitalize on their learnings.
  • Technological Roots: One-third of Citadel’s workforce are software engineers, positioning the firm as a technology company that "manufactures money" rather than a traditional financial institution.

Notable Quotes

"Don't let yourself in management be an impediment to decision-making by those who are junior to you... negative consent to move forward is really powerful because it tells people that they have agency." - on Management Speed
"At Citadel, there's no shame in making money. We manufacture money. Our clients give us capital with one goal in mind: can we manufacture more money with that capital?" - on Firm Purpose
"I want to hear how they're going to climb the next mountain at Citadel... I don't want to hear from somebody who's twenty-two years old that there's some number that represents their magic number [to quit]." - on Hiring and Aspiration
"One of the key challenges for a business is always that dilemma between being on the bleeding edge and being on the leading edge... Pick that path with volition. Don't let that come down to random happenstance." - on Innovation Strategy
Our next speaker started investing from his Harvard dorm room in 1986 and founded one of the world's leading alternative investment firms just a year after graduating. Kenneth Griffin is the founder and CEO of Citadel, which manages over sixty billion dollars in capital. At Citadel, winning is core. They hire the top one percent and create an environment where that top one percent push each other to be even better. How do you build a culture where the best want to stay and compete? Malin Norberg, the Chief Investment Officer for Market Strategies, joins him to find out. Please welcome Kenneth and Malin. Thank you, Ken, for joining us here in Oslo today. I cannot think of a better person to talk to about winning culture. So winning is one of Citadel's core values alongside integrity, learning, meritocracy, and extraordinary colleagues. But winning is the one that stands out there. So why winning? So first, it's just wonderful to be here today. Thank you so much for having me participate in this wonderful conference. And one of the things that is very true is that this team, which has done such an extraordinary job in managing the sovereign wealth of the nation, has been focused on delivering for the people of Norway. You have had a winning team which has really delivered in a profound way for the people of your country. Let me be clear. Why do we actually enumerate winning as one of our core values? Because, regretfully, in the United States, we went through a period of time in which winning seemed to be out of vogue. We lost our way in the wilderness. And great firms are focused on winning. As David Rubenstein put it: can you create a product that the consumer will value, the consumer will pay for, and the consumer will reward you for creating and for building? Firms that are winning firms do just that. And firms that create inferior products are firms that fail. And what worried us is that in a culture where participation trophies were becoming ubiquitous, we lost our way as a country in what it took to win. We at Citadel really do believe in the importance of winning for our clients. That's core to our business. And I was thinking about what David had to say in terms of companies and products. One thing to keep in mind is at Citadel, there's no shame in making money. We manufacture money. Our clients give us capital with one goal in mind: can we manufacture more money with that capital? You certainly manufacture money in a very impressive way. So when it comes to winning culture, Nicolai just mentioned speed as one of the pillars to a winning culture. And your colleague here just told me that one thing that amazes him with Citadel is that you can still move at the speed of a startup even though you are thousands of employees. So how do you do that? So I wish we could. I wish we could. But as you grow, as a business grows in complexity and grows in stakeholders, and grows in regulatory footprint for example, it actually becomes necessary to have more process and more bureaucracy to make sure that what you do is on the right side of the road. Startups have greater flexibility than your established firms. But your established firms have far greater depth and resources. What you have to be very focused on is that you are controlling the size of the bureaucratic state. That you keep a constant focus on the ability to rapidly make decisions and, when a decision is made, to rapidly execute upon that decision. But because you have a bigger footprint, you're still going to have to go through a few more steps than that smaller startup might have to go through. But with more resources, with the ability to interact with other global players on a far stronger playing field, we're able to move quite quickly. On decision-making though, this is one where large firms do tend to find themselves lost in the wilderness. I'll tell my colleagues all the time, when they put something to my desk for input for me, I'll give them a date. I'll be back to you in seventy-two hours, forty-eight hours, or ninety-six hours. If you don't hear from me, I gave you a yes. It's your call. Don't let yourself in management be an impediment to decision-making by those who are junior to you. And in some sense, negative consent to move forward is really powerful because it tells people that they have agency, that they can make a decision. One of our key internal principles is the most junior person who is closest to the information, that's the person who optimally should make the decision as often or as frequently as possible. Now, will they make mistakes? Of course they will. But they will grow much faster in a culture where they're empowered with agency to make decisions. They will learn from their successes and they will learn from their failures. But most importantly, they are rapidly learning. And that's how we create leaders. It's a good point, and talking about developing people, the Wall Street Journal reported that you spend several weeks every year reviewing the performance assessments for thousands of employees. So why is that where you choose to spend your time? And what does it say about the culture at Citadel? Well first of all, I think it says that I deeply care about how my colleagues are advancing in their careers. And what I'm looking for is I'm looking for patterns of where we are not developing talent strongly enough and aggressively enough. So where in the business are our newest hires, whether they're out of college, out of a PhD program, or a lateral hire from another global investment bank or global investment management firm, where are people not flourishing? And I'm trying to understand what do we need to do as a firm, what do we need to do differently, whether it's professional development, whether it's the experiences that people have, or whether it's the trust that is placed in our newer hires. What do we need to do differently to get more out of people? One of the things I'm most proud of at Citadel is the extraordinary amount of human capital that we've developed over thirty-five years. People who run today one of the world's most successful hedge funds, who run one of the most successful securities dealers in the world, and bluntly, countless people who run many of the financial institutions around the world whose roots trace back to Citadel. I think these are all a testament to our consistent focus on developing people to be the best they can be. So Citadel was the pioneer in quantitative investing and you've been using machine learning for well longer than most of us knew what that was. So how do you, with that headstart, ten years of headstart on the AI movement today, how do you make sure you stay ahead? Well, I think that's actually a wonderful story about the kind of firm we want to be. I remember when TensorFlow was released and five of my colleagues came to my office like the day it came out. And they literally said TensorFlow, which was Google's machine learning toolkit, was going to revolutionize the world. Like with complete conviction. And I said okay. And they were basically saying we want to go spend the next couple of weeks really engrossed in what this body of knowledge means and how we can apply it in market making and securities. And I said well I mean of course, like go for it. And really the point of the meeting was not the permission, it was their excitement over this transformative technology. They wanted to bring me behind the curtain to think about what machine learning was going to mean for our business. And I remember calling one of those colleagues about four weeks later and saying, "Okay, so what are your initial findings? Like what have you gleaned so far from using TensorFlow?" He goes, "Well, it's in production." And I'm like, "What do you mean it's in production?" He goes, "Well we used it to quote the US equity portfolio. We've been doing that for the last two weeks." And I'm like, "It's been four weeks since the meeting." He goes, "No no, like a group of us worked pretty much every single day, twelve to fourteen hours a day, because this is one of the coolest things we've ever worked on and this is going to transform the world and we're already using it in production." That's a winning culture. That's a winning culture. And around technology, so with Gen AI technologies, not only for the technologists, but for everyone, have you changed the culture around how you manage technology adoption in the firm? So I think it's important to remember that about one in three people at Citadel are software engineers. So the firm is very, very deeply invested in technology. It's core to what we do. I think it's just important to keep that in the back of your mind when you touch on this topic. So for us with generative AI, I think frankly one of the biggest problems out of the gate was there was perhaps too much enthusiasm for embracing Gen AI. The early generation large language models were really, really fascinating to people because for the first time, machine learning didn't create a vector of zeros and ones, it created written word. You could write a poem, it could write a song, it could write a story, it could write a narrative. I mean, it interacted with people in a way that technology has rarely interacted with individuals. And it was incredibly alluring to people as to how they use this to change what they were doing at work. One of the tougher conversations I had with a colleague is when I said, "Alright, I want a list of all the Gen AI-based projects that we have at Citadel." And he came back with two hundred projects. And I said, "No. No. Five. We're going to do five." And he's like, "Well what about the other one hundred and ninety-five?" I said, "We're going to kill all those. We're going to do five, we're going to actually push them really hard, we're going to see what works and what doesn't work, but we can't afford to let everybody be pursuing random ad hoc projects." It's just too expensive. It's too much of a distraction. Now, of the five that we pursued, we had a real success with just over half. Now of note, the Gen AI models have gotten markedly better. I don't think we incurred any cost of killing one hundred and ninety-five projects. And I do believe that over the course of the last six months with the introduction of OpenAI 5.0, with Claude 4.6, 4.7, I think you have a transformative sea change taking place. You've got many more use cases today that are practical, that are implementable, that are meaningful in impact than you did two years ago. So I think one of the key challenges for a business is always that dilemma between being on the bleeding edge and being on the leading edge. And I think good companies think very long and hard about where is it worth being on the bleeding edge, so it was with TensorFlow. And where do you want to be on the leading edge, where do you want to be a fast follower, where do you want others to sort of pave the way in front of you and you can follow quickly and capitalize on their learnings? I think it's very important in running a big business to think about when do you want to be in the bleeding edge, when do you want to be in the leading edge, and pick that path with volition. Don't let that come down to random happenstance. So one of the places where you're in the leading edge is hiring the top people. So you have hundreds of thousands of applications every year and you've said Citadel hires winners in life and not just winners in business. So say I'm applying for a job at Citadel and I'm sitting next to you for a job interview. So what's the one question you would ask me to see if I was the right cultural fit for Citadel? Oh, I wish one question would do the trick. What's the most essential question? Look, I don't think there's not one; it's a mosaic. Okay? We're all people, we're complicated. And what you want to understand is what is the mosaic of this person and how does that fit into our team holistically and the team they're going to be a part of. So there are a few things that I'm deeply interested in in learning about you. Nicolai, you spoke about ambition. I actually prefer the word aspiration over ambition because I think it appeals to our deeper inner meaning more directly. What are we aspiring to do in this world? What are we aspiring to create or to change? I want to know your ambitions or aspirations. I want to know what success looks like to you. You know, I had a young man from Harvard with me years ago and I asked him right to the point: if you made ten million dollars, what would you do? And he goes, "I would quit and I would climb the highest peaks around the world." I said okay. I said I don't think this is the right firm for you. And he goes, "Well you've already made an offer to me." I said, "That's wonderful, I strongly urge you not to accept it." Because I don't want to hear from somebody who's twenty-two years old that there's some number that represents their magic number—that if they made that much money, they're going to climb mountains around the world. I want to hear how they're going to climb the next mountain at Citadel. How they're going to grow a business for us, how they're going to change the firm. How they want to lead our Asian business. Or look, if I made ten million dollars, I'd like to think that it was done because I was very successful at X and that I'd have more responsibility here to have a bigger impact on the firm. That's what I want to hear. Right? So I look for people's aspirations. I look for their track record of being a winner. You know, I love people who competed in competitive sports in high school and college. They're not going to play tennis for Citadel's tennis team. But their willingness to get up every single day and go to practice, their willingness to put it all out, in this case on the court, and to win or to be beaten says a lot about their resiliency and their perseverance. And those are really important life skills that you learn in playing competitive sports. In fact, in choosing to compete in any arena—speech and debate, as a musician—competing in any arena tells me that you are willing to win and to lose. And that is such an important life skill. People forget that my average equity portfolio manager is right in about fifty-three percent of their stock picks. Okay? Could you imagine you're going to your surgeon and he goes, "Yeah, about fifty-three percent of my patients live"? What a humbling job. You're right about fifty-three percent of the time. If you're right about fifty-three percent of the time with the right upside-downside ratio, you're actually extraordinarily good as a portfolio manager. But that means you go home a lot of days really wrong and really humbled. Do you have the mental fortitude to come back to work the next day and get right back at it? Well, I think those are some great advice and insights to a winning culture and I think that's a good note to end on. So thank you so much Ken, this has been very insightful.

TL;DR At the 2026 annual meeting, CEO Greg Abel demonstrated a seamless leadership transition while preserving Berkshire Hathaway’s core culture of extreme decentralization, reporting a robust financial foundation with $380 billion in net cash, a landmark strategic partnership...

TL;DR

At the 2026 annual meeting, CEO Greg Abel demonstrated a seamless leadership transition while preserving Berkshire Hathaway’s core culture of extreme decentralization, reporting a robust financial foundation with $380 billion in net cash, a landmark strategic partnership with Tokio Marine, and a disciplined approach to integrating technology and "Narrow AI" across operations.

Leadership Transition and Core Culture

  • CEO Transition Validation: Warren Buffett praised CEO Greg Abel's performance, declaring that Abel has successfully assumed all operational duties and is executing them more effectively than Buffett did himself.
  • Retiring Jerseys: In a symbolic nod to their legacy, Berkshire retired jersey number 60 for Warren Buffett (marking his 60 years as CEO) alongside Charlie Munger’s jersey number 45.
  • CFO Succession: Long-standing CFO Marc Hamburg will retire in June 2027 after 40 years at Berkshire. He will transition his responsibilities over the next year to an incoming CFO and a newly appointed general counsel.
  • Cultural Bedrock: Abel re-emphasized the "newspaper test" as Berkshire's ultimate compliance standard, referring to Buffett's famous 1991 Salomon Brothers congressional testimony on safeguarding reputation above capital.

Financial Position and Capital Allocation

  • Massive Cash Reserves: Berkshire reported gross cash and U.S. Treasuries of $397.4 billion. Adjusted for $17.2 billion in end-of-quarter payables, the net cash position stands at $380 billion. Total cash and investments reached $705.8 billion.
  • Tokio Marine Alliance: Reinsurance head Ajit Jain outlined a major three-part transaction with Tokio Marine & Fire:
    • Equity Stake: A $1.8 billion purchase representing a 2.5% ownership stake.
    • Underwriting Slice: A multi-year quota-share agreement on Tokio Marine’s highly profitable property-casualty book.
    • Strategic Partnership: A broad, open-ended agreement to collaborate on global M&A and operational insurance opportunities.
  • Capital Deployments: Major Q1 capital moves included $235 million in Berkshire stock repurchases and $9.5 billion in cash outflows to close the Occidental Petroleum (OxyChem) transaction.
  • The Conglomerate Advantage: Abel strongly defended Berkshire's conglomerate model, highlighting its lack of bureaucratic corporate layers and its unique ability to transfer capital tax-efficiently between operating businesses, equities, and cash.

Insurance Performance and Underwriting Discipline

  • Benign Catastrophic Environment: A quiet quarter for natural disasters allowed Berkshire's insurance segments to post stellar combined ratios, with primary insurance at 87% and reinsurance at 89.6%, far outperforming the 10-year historical average of 93%.
  • GEICO’s Balancing Act: Under CEO Nancy Pierce, GEICO logged an exceptional 87.3% combined ratio (~12% operating profit margin) driven by years of pricing risk adjustments. However, GEICO faces growth challenges:
    • Growth Discrepancy: GEICO's policies in force grew by only 2% year-over-year, lagging far behind rival Progressive's 11% growth.
    • Future Mandate: Pierce is focusing on customer retention and reviving GEICO's growth engine as competitors aggressively target its policyholders.
  • Stance on Cyber Risk: Ajit Jain reaffirmed Berkshire's hesitation to aggressively underwrite cyber insurance, citing the virtual impossibility of modeling aggregate risk and the decline of premium rates due to excess industry capital.

Rail, Energy, and Industrial Operations

  • BNSF Railway Improvements: Led by CEO Katie Farmer, BNSF raised its operating margin by 250 basis points to 34.5%, lifting the railway from 5th to 4th among the six Class 1 U.S. railroads. Management remains committed to closing the efficiency gap with leader Union Pacific (39.5%).
  • PacifiCorp Legal Victory: In a massive development for Berkshire Hathaway Energy (BHE), an Oregon appellate court reversed and remanded a costly class-action wildfire liability verdict, allowing BHE to recover $1 billion in posted bond security.
  • Regulatory Compact and Washington Divestment: Abel stressed the necessity of a balanced regulatory compact to protect capital returns. Consequently, BHE is selling its Washington State utility operations due to state policies that shifted unfair costs onto customers in other states.
  • Aerospace Surge: Precision Castparts (led by Mark Donegan) and international toolmaker IMC (led by Jacob Harpaz) are experiencing a historic 10-year commercial aviation backlog as airlines aggressively phase out older, less fuel-efficient planes.
  • Affordable Housing Innovation: Clayton Homes is leveraging factory manufacturing to deliver "CrossMod" homes (70% factory-built, 30% site-built) starting at $249,000 including the lot, alongside single-wide models for under $35,000.

The Custom Technology and "Narrow AI" Blueprint

  • "Builder" vs. "Buyer" Pivot: Based on a technology transformation framework designed at GEICO, Berkshire is pivoting from buying fragmented software applications to hiring in-house engineers to build custom, integrated software systems.
  • "Narrow AI" Governance: Abel introduced three strict principles guiding Berkshire's rollout of artificial intelligence (which he prefers to label "Large Logic Models"):
    • Human Involvement: Senior managers and employees must remain actively in the loop to implement and authorize AI-generated recommendations.
    • Consistency Safeguards: Queries run on constrained, secure datasets must yield identical, repeatable results over time to ensure data integrity.
    • Tangible Value Propositions: Berkshire will strictly avoid "AI for the sake of AI," demanding clear operational and efficiency gains before funding tech projects.

If I start by looking at these results, I'll start with the insurance, total insurance. I'm starting there because there are a couple of really important points to make. You can see that in the first quarter of 2026, we're actually up quarter-on-quarter. And yet, in the letter that I sent out just in February, I highlighted the fact that we are unlikely to see stronger results in 2026. But there are some important points here. In 2025, we had an 860-million-dollar after-tax charge associated with California wildfires that we insured. The adjustment is not so important; it's just to highlight that for the 2026 results and what we're feeling in the insurance industry right now, there are two things. First, our 2026 results do not reflect any catastrophic events. It was a pretty benign period; there were some storms in the northeast part of the United States, but relative to 2025 and past years, it was very benign. And that highlights again that for our insurance business, but also the industry as a whole, the pricing—we've talked about that hardening, i.e., can you get the proper premium for risk—is becoming a more challenging market. What's driving that? When you see a benign environment, and I'll touch on further results in another layer, you start to see competition coming into the industry. They bring a variety of products and forms, but really, they're bringing capital into the industry. So, I just wanted to highlight that we still see that as a softening market, and I'll expand on that. Now, when we discuss insurance, we have two core objectives at Berkshire. We want to underwrite at a profit—so, create a profit ultimately for our shareholders—but truly underwrite at a combined ratio. I'll come back to that, because there's a lot of insurance jargon here and there are a lot of numbers. And then the second core objective is to increase our float. Now for the insurance jargon and the numbers. Go to the combined ratio on the left and go way over to the right to the 10-year average of 93%. I'll give you a little bit of color here. If we have a $100 premium that comes in associated with a policy, the 93% represents the cost incurred associated with that premium. It can be the cost of writing the premium, the commissions, or the loss reserve we set up. The 7%, i.e., $7 on a hundred dollars, is our operating profit on that premium. And then, if I go back to the $93, just roughly $23 of that would be the expenses, the administration of running the business. That's not just an average; it varies across our businesses, and it varies across the industry. The other $70—and this is very important—is actually what goes down into float. So, when you see our float growing, we take that $70, it goes into our float, and we'll incur claims against it over the years to come. When the claim shows up, we pay out against that 70. If you take a simple small commercial business or personal insurance, that $70 effectively gets paid out likely over a three-to-four-year period. That premium sits there as float. We earn on it, as Warren has referenced many, many times; it's a valued part of Berkshire, and it's really the opportunity to continue to create value for you as our owners and shareholders. You can see on the float, going back to 2015, it has effectively doubled through 2025. There's a small increase in the current quarter, and while I wanted you all to see it, it could have just as easily decreased because it's subject to the payment cycle we're in. The really core and important objective is that we grow it over the long run, and we'll continue to provide those types of updates to you as our owners. Now, if I go back up to the underwriting results and you see the combined ratio again, we'll focus on 2026. Primary and reinsurance are both 87% and 89.6%. The amazing thing there is that there's an eight in that number. Again, our 10-year average is in the 93% range, so we're actually realizing more operating insurance income profit there. Again, what's driving that? It is a very benign environment when you think of the catastrophic environment we insure into. The last time there was a hurricane that made landfall in the US was 19 months ago. So, our quarterly results and our results last year do not reflect those types of outcomes. Again, that means we have more capital coming into the industry. Yes, we like those results, but the reality is that as our insurance business softens and we cannot realize the value we should for the related risk, Ajit and our insurance team across the businesses will start writing less premium. We still want to write it at an underwriting profit because there are still opportunities there and a number of risks we will insure, but we'll be much more cautious, specifically across the primary and reinsurance businesses. Now let's move to GEICO. As I highlighted, we have a new CEO, and we're fortunate to have Nancy leading that team. They have an even better combined ratio of 87.3%. That means, associated with that business, there is 12% plus operating income coming off of each dollar of premium we write there. That is an exceptional result. What has driven that is that four or five years ago, the GEICO team stepped back and said they felt they weren't, relative to the risk, getting the proper premium and the proper price for risk. Over the last four years, we have worked hard; the GEICO team worked hard to get the proper balance across that. That meant our premiums went up for our customers across certain classes of drivers. We worked hard to segment those customers, and by the way, that happened across the auto industry generally speaking. You saw an increase in the overall premium as they managed that underlying risk. Again, what does that mean to the industry, and what does that mean to GEICO? Well, for one, there are a lot of folks out there pursuing those customers. Anytime you increase a customer's insurance premium, especially over a period of time—and I'm talking about both GEICO and our competitors—people start evaluating and shopping. We've seen unprecedented shopping activity across the auto space, and you see the advertising that's out there. They are pursuing customers, and they are pursuing the GEICO customer. So yes, there is an important balance I want to highlight to all of you, and this is what our GEICO team is working on. Yes, we have to get the price to risk right, but there are two other important things we really need to balance. The second piece is we really do want to retain our customers. There is no more valued customer than our GEICO customers. Many of you as shareholders and owners of Berkshire are GEICO customers; we want to retain all of you, and we want to retain every GEICO customer. So, as we found that right price to risk, the next challenge is making sure we retain our customers, and Nancy and her team have that as a clear objective and are working hard on that. The third piece of that balance is to grow GEICO. How do we measure growth in that industry? It's policies in force. If I touch on what we've experienced as growth in GEICO, going back to last quarter of 2025 versus this quarter ending March of this year, our policies in force grew by 2%. Now, compare that to the number one competitor in our industry, Progressive. They just announced their first-quarter results, and they grew by 11%. Our team at GEICO fully acknowledges that balance they have to find across those three metrics, including growth. It's not going to be easy to just restart the growth engine; we acknowledge that. But they understand the objective, and as we go through '26 and into 2027, the two important objectives they have are to retain our customers and start growing GEICO again. The last thing I'll touch on in the insurance business is Tokyo Marine. I'm not going to expand a lot other than to say we announced the transaction in the fourth week of March. It was a great transaction by Ajit and his team, and it's a strategic transaction. I'm highlighting that because, yes, there is a financial aspect of it, and we're thrilled with that, but it is a long-term strategic partnership. When Ajit is on stage, I'll have him expand on that. So, well done, Ajit, and thank you to you and your team for a great, great transaction for Berkshire. Now I'll move to our non-insurance businesses. I'll start with BNSF. As I've highlighted, a number of our non-insurance businesses provide critical products and services, and BNSF is a great example of that. It has 32,500 miles of track in the West, moving core products for a number of customers that touch every industry in the US. You'll see some improvement in the results there. But what we really want to highlight today—and Katie will be joining me on stage and she'll touch on this—is that we have a lot of work we know we need to do at BNSF. We have a great group of employees who have been working very hard on the ground, boots on the ground. You've heard me talk in the past that we have to work hard in our yards, working on how we can move our cars quicker and meet our customers' expectations. We're doing a very good job on the customer service side, but we've recognized we've got to get better operationally. Our team has also been very focused on what resources we have. Do we have too many locomotives? Too many locomotives actually sounds counterintuitive, but it can be a problem; you are not as efficient, and congestion and everything else comes with it. So, our team has been very focused on that. And then, how do we best use our employees? That's something our team is working hard on, as we work to become more efficient and more effective. We also have to very much recognize where we are versus our industry peers. There are six Class 1 railroads that operate in the US, and we're one of them. You can see that last year we were fifth out of six, and that's the reality of where we are. But we are also getting better, and we are going to get better. We recognize that despite our teams working hard, there is a lot of room for improvement. Now, the good news is, if I look at 2025, what we have here is our operating margin. The 34.5% you see for BNSF is the operating profit that came back associated with its underlying operations. That operating margin improved by two and a half percent, or 250 basis points. That's a very positive outcome, and in fairness to our team, that's the work they've been putting in. On a nominal basis, that was the largest improvement across our five peers. So, we're pleased with that, but we know there's a lot of work to be done. If you look at our first-quarter results, I'm happy to report that we went from fifth to fourth, but our team would be the first to say there's a lot more to be done. If you look at our overall operating margin, it is very consistent with the result last year, and the efficiency we've delivered is being maintained and improved over the first quarter of 2025. So again, we see a lot of opportunity here to continue to get better. But to achieve where, say, Union Pacific is as the leader with an operating margin of 39.5%, we know that's going to require a step-change in how we operate and even how we approach our operations. An important step we've identified as we look to find the gaps and see where we can get better is technology. We're doing a lot at BNSF, and I'll touch on our other businesses here when I go through technology. But that's where we see a step-change, or potentially where a few of our peers have gapped out versus how we're using technology. I'm going to back up to GEICO and then I'll come back to BNSF. Approximately four years ago, I was in a GEICO meeting with our management team, and they were discussing this price-to-risk and segmenting customers. We had our operational team from GEICO, we had the commercial team, and we had the tech team there. They're often there when you're looking for some help, but what I heard in that discussion was a clear technology transformation that was happening at GEICO. It was obvious that technology was going to be a big part of the solution as GEICO tackled their challenges. As that meeting wrapped up, I very much wanted to spend more time with the technology team to understand what was driving this. They were calling it a technology transformation, and I could see that it was so applicable to what we were going to do, what we needed to do, and what we would pursue across our non-insurance businesses. So, what is this technology transformation that they described at GEICO? I'll summarize it in a few different ways. First and foremost, we recognized we were going to become a builder of technology rather than just a buyer of technology. Previously, we had a number of systems, and we often bought the related applications or software that came with them. While they were valued applications, they were disconnected from our systems, and we didn't have the ability to easily use the information and get to the data. What they started to talk about is simplifying the infrastructure, making sure we would build what we needed ourselves to deliver solutions back to our customers, and ensuring we would have clear access to the data. These are all things that make a lot of sense, but it is a massive challenge. It doesn't happen overnight, and we're still on that journey at GEICO in year five—there's no question. We quickly recognized that this could be used across our other businesses. We were very fortunate that GEICO had put a leadership team in place to drive forward this transformation. The most senior leader from GEICO then joined our non-insurance operations, taking on a senior leadership role helping us with the technology transformation at Berkshire Hathaway Energy, and also serving as the senior technology officer at BNSF. So, we've started down that journey. One of the first things you have to do is say, "OK, we need a different resource base." Now, we're hiring engineers and developers in our technology group to help us start to build the solutions we need for these businesses, and it's going beyond GEICO now. We still have our valued employees there, and they may be retraining or transitioning to other roles. But the reality is we need fewer people managing applications and software, and more people building the outcomes that our businesses need. Now, when I asked our team how AI—artificial intelligence—fits into this, it's actually a big piece because it effectively sits on top of a lot of our systems. That's what they're building; they're using AI to build applications. That is all great, but we also know there are certain risks around humanity, broader risks globally, and risks for our country. There are also risks within our businesses. As they started heading down this path, I asked, "How should we think about this? How can we all be comfortable that we're approaching this correctly?" They said they don't really like to call it artificial intelligence; they call it narrow artificial intelligence. And they have three really important principles associated with it. The first principle is that, yes, we are using it, and we'll use it with the engineers we have and the highly skilled individuals we've brought in, but how are we going to manage it? Well, we still have our employees and our senior management team involved in implementing the recommendations that we receive from the architecture or framework we've put in place. As decisions move up and the important choices are being made, there is human involvement. Our managers and employees are involved, and that is part of the governance that is in place. The second piece is what they call the safeguard. The safeguard is very intriguing, because right away, of course, we all want good governance, but what does that actually mean? Our team said, "Here's how I would describe it: if we ask for an outcome, a recommendation, or an action right now, and then half an hour later we ask again, do we get the exact same outcome?" If we receive that same outcome, that is effectively the safeguard. It means we are utilizing that application properly and, importantly, it means we have a defined data set that we're comfortable with. I like to call it the constraint. We know we're constraining our data; we know what data we're using, and we know what data is coming in. Now, when you talk about all the operations we're focused on, yes, the next day of operations comes in and updates that data set. If we ask the question a day later, we might get a marginally different answer because it has new information. But if we ignore today's information and just focus on yesterday's, do we get the same answer? Yes. So, we call that our safeguard. The third thing on technology associated with this narrow AI is that it has to be additive to our businesses. We're not going to do AI for the sake of AI. You can spend a lot of money in this area, so we need to know what we're trying to achieve and whether we see a value proposition for the businesses. That's what we call narrow AI, and if you see how it's starting to be applied at BNSF, it is incredible. Think of BNSF: we have the expansive network I touched on, and we have a variety of trains leaving from various points every day. It can be the intermodal trains of 150 to 200 on our tracks a day, which are moving very quickly and often leaving LA to deliver product in Chicago 48 hours later. In the last quarter, we had more than 750 trains a day moving across that system. There is weather, there are equipment failures, and we share our tracks, allowing Amtrak to use them, meaning they can be running on time or running behind schedule. We have to adjust to all of that. The reality is Katie and her team have a system that has been running for 177 years, but we were not where we needed to be in how we use technology to operate it better. That is what we are using now, or starting down the path of using, and we know we'll see a step-change in our operating performance because of it. To summarize all that: I want to let you know it's all around operational excellence. We are going to get better at rail, but we're also going to use that framework across all of our businesses. They will create the framework, and then our teams can embrace it if they so choose. We will help them see the value of it, but there is a real opportunity there. I'll break it down with one last comment around technology. When you think of AI, or artificial intelligence, everybody talks about the large language models, learning models, and there is a lot more to it than what I just highlighted. But I summarize it as one thing, and this is why there is an opportunity across all of our businesses: those large language models—I communicate them to our teams as, or at least it helps me understand them as—large logic models. At this point in time, we're using them to solve logical challenges in our business in a more efficient fashion; i.e., doing it more quickly and getting to a better answer. If I move to energy now and provide an update there, I'm just going to touch on the opportunity first and then come to the challenges. As I've just discussed, technology is the opportunity in energy. One of the core inputs to all those data centers and hyperscalers associated with artificial intelligence is energy. Our businesses have that opportunity right in front of them at Berkshire Hathaway Energy. Yes, we are pursuing them, and we'll do it in a way we view as the right approach for both our states and our customers. But I would highlight that this is not new to us. If you just go across the river a little bit east to Iowa, we serve just under 50% of that state. If you look at the number of data centers and hyperscalers in that state, it is very significant. We have four very large hyperscaler data centers there—or builders of them—and ultimately their customers using it. But if I look at our peak load—i.e., the amount of energy being used by those data centers—it is at 8% of our peak load. The only reason I highlight that 8% is because when I hear people in the industry, all the utilities around us, and a lot of states, they're talking about this great opportunity. Hopefully, in the next five years, starting from a relatively low point, they want to get to 5% to 10%. We are already at 8%, and we see opportunities to grow that by 50% over the next five years, or potentially more. We will do it in a way—and you're starting to hear more and more of this across the US—where we're not going to impact the costs of our other customers. The users of the energy, meaning the hyperscalers and the data centers, have to bear their full costs. We cannot transfer that burden across all our other customers, and that's a principle we've applied across all our utilities from the very early goings of building these data centers. I would highlight that I think our team is doing an exceptional job of that. If I again go back to MidAmerican, with all the data centers and hyperscalers and the infrastructure they've put in place, their rates are still 45% below the national average. That is just unheard of. It is an exceptional outcome, and it highlights that they're doing the right things when they build this infrastructure. We would highlight that we have similar positive outcomes across the rest of our utilities. Now, I would note one other thing: our gas network and infrastructure. For our large pipeline company, as they build out all this infrastructure—not just in our utilities but across the US—our pipeline footprint will grow. A lot of it is being built with natural gas, and we'll meet that challenge. Here is an interesting point: 15% of the natural gas consumed in the United States is touched by our pipeline network or one of our core assets there. So, again, that is the opportunity on the energy side. But it's not without its challenges, and we've talked about this the past few years. When I think of energy and the Berkshire Hathaway Energy Group, what is the challenge? It's what I call the regulatory compact. We leave your capital—our owners' capital, Berkshire's capital—in these businesses, and often a portion of the earnings that they generate we reinvest back into those businesses. For that, we get a very specific set of returns. Over the long run, it has been a very balanced and fair return, but how do you measure that? It's measured against the risks we take on in that business, and that's the compact. They are going to pay us X percent return, and in exchange, what risks are they asking us to take? That model has worked very well for a number of years and for centuries, but the problem is it's becoming more stressed. If you think of inflation or the data center challenges—though I strongly believe we're managing that separately—and then you move to assets that are 60 to 100 years old that are starting to retire as we bring new ones into the network. The challenge is to get more efficient and more effective from the operational side every day, but as a regulator or a governor, you're very focused on not wanting rates to go up and not wanting to take on more risk. They want to transfer that back to us. That's the regulatory compact, and unless that balance exists, we do not deploy our capital back into those businesses or into those utilities. We work hard to maintain it. There has been a very important challenge we've had within that, which we've touched on the past two years, and that's wildfires. Wildfires in the West have been very prevalent the last 15-plus years, particularly in California. We experienced a very significant wildfire in Oregon in 2020—actually, a number of wildfires across Oregon, affecting both the state and the company. So, there were a number of wildfires across the state. We had certain equipment, high winds, and certain failures with our equipment that contributed to those fires. Associated with that, we've fully acknowledged where there was causation and where we were responsible for it. But there was also, associated with some of the fires and specifically one fire, a class-action lawsuit that had very large claims against our utility there, PacifiCorp. We had to approach it such that we'd resolve all the other matters, but this was a class action, and there was specifically one fire that we strongly felt we weren't responsible for. There was zero causation. There was an Oregon Forestry Department report that said PacifiCorp did not contribute to nor cause the fire. We took a very strong position there: first, we were not going to put more capital in to fund the entity for these types of risks and obligations; and second, we would challenge that liability verdict. We challenged it, and it has been a long process. But for our owners and shareholders, this was a very significant event that occurred in this past quarter, or specifically in April: we are very fortunate that the appellate court reversed and remanded that liability verdict and said, "Back to ground zero, start over again." What they were really saying was that that class of customers—whom did we actually affect, where was the causation—will be revisited, along with the related damages. There are some positive things associated with this: we recover a billion dollars of security we've already posted. The law firms that pursued it are responsible for our costs associated with that period of time—not our litigation costs, but the costs we incurred in posting the bonds and posting that security, which is around $10 million, or approaching likely $10 million. But the most important thing is we've reset the stage there. That's very important, because we're working hard to get that regulatory compact balanced and to get the right outcome. We do want to see these utilities move forward, and we want to be a very good operator and steward of those assets for our customers. One last thing I'll touch on regarding wildfires: when you think of PacifiCorp, yes, we've addressed that challenge, but to get the right compact, we've worked with Wyoming, Idaho, and Utah. I've touched on Utah on this stage to say it requires a judicial system that supports the legislature and the laws in place. More importantly, we also need good legislation that sets that balance. We've had it across those states, and we'll continue to work hard across our other states. So, it is an exceptional outcome, and I wanted to make sure you know there's still a lot to be done there because we're back to first base on the legal proceedings. Okay, so cyber is something we worry about in the insurance operation at two levels. Firstly, there is a huge demand by people in business all over the world who are interested in buying protection against some kind of a cyber incident. We have been slow, in terms of—consciously, we have been slow in terms of entering that class of business as an underwriter. The reason for that is: firstly, on cyber, I find it very difficult to have some meaningful method to assess and model the aggregation. People will tell you we've got it under control and they'll show you all kinds of models, but nothing that I can really hang my hat on in terms of we really have a good feeling for what the aggregate exposure is. Because any risk we take on, the first question we ask ourselves: how bad can bad be? And I'm not sure we can answer that question as well as we should. So the second reason is: cyber's been a very popular, fashionable product in these last several years; we have not played in it. Now, as it turns out, there haven't been very many cyber losses, so people who've taken on cyber risk have actually made profits, and as a result of which the premiums that cyber insurance commands have been coming down over time. So we'd hate entering a line of business where prices are coming down. So we're sort of sitting on the sidelines, and I'm not sure when, but I'm pretty certain that the day will come when we will have a fairly significant role to play in cyber. Secondly, you know, we being a large company are exposed to cyber perils ourselves. We try and do the best we can. We, I think, are as good as anyone else. Now, cyber insurance is very highly regulated by the regulators, and we've been consistently above what the regulations call for. The 32 companies in the portfolio—consumer products, services, and retail—it's a stat that I love. They've actually been around on average 88 years, and only 0.5% of American businesses have been around more than 80 years. Our average in that sector, from a founding standpoint, is 88 years. And several of the—five of the companies, specifically—are companies that were founded in the 1800s. And when I called those CEOs, they said, "We've been dealing with tariffs for 100 years," you know, kind of thing. And so, not being dismissive at all of tariffs, the point is, I look at the whole tariff conversation as: you're always going to have a curveball. If I think of the CEOs in the last, you know, seven, eight years, we've had to deal with a global pandemic, the highest inflation in 40 years, and now this thing, you know, the bouncing ball of tariffs. So, the businesses have done an excellent job of managing through that. I wouldn't put it in the fun department of the things we have to deal with, but we're learning it, and I think we're in a pretty decent spot moving forward. So... Thank you, Adam. We'll move to station six. Good afternoon. My name is Amir Reihani from Vancouver, Canada. Thank you for hosting us, and thanks to everyone at headquarters that makes this weekend possible. Yes, we like the 2.5% investment in Tokio Marine, and that will be a long-term investment. It's the type of investment we put with our other five investments in Japan. We really think of those as forever because it goes beyond the investment, and it's very much around the relationships we want to build there. And you'll continue to see that. Now to the really tough question: Canada versus US in hockey. I did find a way—it can cause a lot of angst in my own family. So, I remember waking up that morning, and Canada was playing the men. But I'd already decided a little bit earlier that when it came to the Canadian men versus the US men, Connor McDavid plays for Edmonton, and therefore, I was in a check because, being from Edmonton, I would cheer for the Canadian men's team.
